        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177"><meta property="og:title" content="Mechanistic Insight into Ultrafast Kinetics of Sodium Cointercalation in Few-Layer Graphitic Carbon"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/asset/images/acs.nanolett.2c02177.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Fast-charging sodium ion batteries remain deeply challenged by the lack of suitable carbonaceous anodes that exhibit intercalation plateau with fast kinetics. Here we develop a few-layer graphitic carbon with nanoscale architecture, which enables shortened Na+ ion diffusion path and fast formation of fully intercalated phase at the same time. Combined in situ Raman and electrochemical test reveal that this graphitic carbon with highly crystalline few layers follows surface-controlled intercalation rather than typical diffusion-controlled kinetics observed in natural graphite. As a result, a few-layer graphitic carbon anode maintains the reversible capacity of 106 mAh g–1 at 10 A g–1 and achieves 87% capacity retention even after 10 000 cycles at 1 A g–1. This work provides new insight on the Na storage mechanism in fast-charging graphitic carbon as well as the design of carbon anodes for high-rate sodium ion batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/asset/images/acs.nanolett.2c02177.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93187ed0a9d1075b-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187ed0a9d1075b-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'c55f9681-247e-4d16-b7d7-93b970dfda6a'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187ed0a9d1075b-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano_letters';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano_letters/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano_letters/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

data-getftr-references-enabled="data-getFTR-references-enabled" data-getftr-reference-url="/action/CitationEntitlement" data-getftr-citedby-enabled="data-getFTR-citedBy-enabled" data-references="https://pubs.acs.org/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.2c02177&amp;ajax=true" data-enable-mathjax="true" vocab="http://schema.org/" typeof="ScholarlyArticle" class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite This:</strong></span></a><span class="article_header-cite-this figure-viewer-cite-this"><span class="cit-fg-title"><i>Nano Lett.</i></span><span <script nonce="93187ed0a9d1075b-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "nalefd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mechanistic Insight into Ultrafast Kinetics of Sodium Cointercalation in Few-Layer Graphitic Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mechanistic Insight into Ultrafast Kinetics of Sodium Cointercalation in Few-Layer Graphitic Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mechanistic Insight into Ultrafast Kinetics of Sodium Cointercalation in Few-Layer Graphitic Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mechanistic Insight into Ultrafast Kinetics of Sodium Cointercalation in Few-Layer Graphitic Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mechanistic Insight into Ultrafast Kinetics of Sodium Cointercalation in Few-Layer Graphitic Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mechanistic Insight into Ultrafast Kinetics of Sodium Cointercalation in Few-Layer Graphitic Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mechanistic Insight into Ultrafast Kinetics of Sodium Cointercalation in Few-Layer Graphitic Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mechanistic Insight into Ultrafast Kinetics of Sodium Cointercalation in Few-Layer Graphitic Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Mechanistic Insight into Ultrafast Kinetics of Sodium Cointercalation in Few-Layer Graphitic Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/asset/images/medium/nl2c02177_0005.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/asset/images/large/nl2c02177_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Fast-charging sodium ion batteries remain deeply challenged by the lack of suitable carbonaceous anodes that exhibit intercalation plateau with fast kinetics. Here we develop a few-layer graphitic carbon with nanoscale architecture, which enables shortened Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion path and fast formation of fully intercalated phase at the same time. Combined in situ Raman and electrochemical test reveal that this graphitic carbon with highly crystalline few layers follows surface-controlled intercalation rather than typical diffusion-controlled kinetics observed in natural graphite. As a result, a few-layer graphitic carbon anode maintains the reversible capacity of 106 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> and achieves 87% capacity retention even after 10 000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. This work provides new insight on the Na storage mechanism in fast-charging graphitic carbon as well as the design of carbon anodes for high-rate sodium ion batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D6359%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DJiali%2BWang%252C%2BHuwei%2BWang%252C%2BRongyi%2BZhao%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D22%26issueNum%3D15%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.2c02177%26title%3DMechanistic%2BInsight%2Binto%2BUltrafast%2BKinetics%2Bof%2BSodium%2BCointercalation%2Bin%2BFew-Layer%2BGraphitic%2BCarbon%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D6365%26publicationDate%3DAugust%2B2022" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2022 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291337&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon"> Carbon </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291064&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Intercalation"> Intercalation </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290667&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Ions"> Ions </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290905&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Kinetics"> Kinetics </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Few-layer+Graphitic+Carbon&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_few-layer%20graphitic%20carbon">few-layer graphitic carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Surface-controlled+Intercalation&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_surface-controlled%20intercalation">surface-controlled intercalation</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Fast+Charging&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_fast%20charging">fast charging</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><p class="first">Fast charging capability is regarded as one of the most economically useful benefits of sodium ion batteries (SIBs), while insufficient electronic conductivity and ionic diffusion have been identified as primary challenges for the development of SIB anodes in fast charging conditions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> Although some encouraging advances have been made in developing suitable carbonaceous anodes for SIBs, few can fulfill the requirement for fast charging. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4,5)</a> Specifically, porous carbon and defect-rich graphene are the two main anodes for fast charging. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6,7)</a> Both pores and defects in structure contribute to the Na-ion adsorption, which is regarded as the enhanced pseudocapacitance/double-layer capacitance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8,9)</a> Meanwhile, this is generally thought to be the mainstream mechanism for fast-charging carbon anodes. That is, Na-ion diffusion in these carbons is efficient enough, surface-controlled process thus normally dominates the Na-ion storage behavior. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> However, as the anode, the output voltages achieved in these carbons are far from ideal due to their slope-like discharge/charge feature without intercalation plateau. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 11 and 12">(11,12)</a></p><p>On the other hand, although graphite anode follows ternary Faradaic intercalation with potential plateau region in the sodiation profile, its micrometer-size is unfavorable for ionic diffusion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 13 to 15">(13−15)</a> The graphite carbon thus needs to be further optimized for fast charging. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> Therefore, a promising anode that exhibits potential plateau and ensures fast diffusion kinetics at the same time is highly desirable. Meanwhile, for the reported fast-charging carbon anodes for SIBs, Na-ion storage mechanism basically follows that of Li counterparts, where either electrochemical capacitance behavior or Faradaic intercalation has long been acknowledged. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 17 and 18">(17,18)</a> However, the detailed understanding on fast Na-ion storage mechanisms especially in kinetics is rarely addressed.</p><p>Herein, a few-layer graphitic carbon (FGC) for fast Na storage is proposed, which enables superior rate performance (106 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>) and excellent cycling stability (10 000-cycle with 87% capacity retention at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>) in the ether-based electrolyte. Electrochemical tests and in situ measurements were combined to elucidate that a rapid ternary Faradaic intercalation is achieved in the FGC, where a clear potential plateau with clear phase transition can be identified. Meanwhile, different from the diffusion-controlled process followed by the conventional micrometer-size graphite anode, surface-controlled kinetics is first verified in the nanosized FGC. This leads us to provide additional insight on the Na storage mechanism in fast-charging graphitic carbon, namely, high crystalline few-layer nanostructure contributes to fast diffusion kinetics, and thus fast intercalation behavior.</p><p>FGC was synthesized by oxidizing graphite using modified Hummers’ method <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> and followed by two-step heat treatment, which are widely used in laboratory and industry. Specifically, during the graphite oxide precursor synthesis procedure, sonication was deliberately avoided. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> The two-step heat treatment and structure evolution of FGC are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>. In the first carbonization process at 1000 °C, the loss of oxygen-containing groups generated local high pressure to overcome the van der Waals force between graphene sheets, enabling the separation of carbon layers. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> However, it is inevitable to produce large amounts of defects during the annealing process, resulting in the decreasing of graphitization degree (broad XRD peak around 25°, high <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value, and large surface area in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). To obtain defect-free carbon structure, heat treatment at higher temperatures (1400, 2200, and 3000 °C) was used to further reconstruct the carbon. After the two-step thermal treatment, the residual oxygen of carbonized product at 1000 °C completely disappear (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). As probed by transmission electron microscopy (TEM) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>), all three FGC samples (X in “FGC-X” denotes the graphitization temperatures) are 2D layered structures with different surface morphology. Meanwhile, the surface features of samples were characterized by atomic force microscopy (AFM). As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>, the abrupt changes of height in FGC1400 and FGC3000 suggest nonuniform wrinkles form along the surface. In contrast, FGC2200 shows the smallest thickness and the flattest surface (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a). This uniform surface obtained in FGC2200 was further confirmed by measuring the thickness of numerous FGC samples (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5c</a>).</p><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/asset/images/medium/nl2c02177_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/asset/images/large/nl2c02177_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Morphological and structural characterization. (a) AFM image of FGC2200 with uniform nanometer-thickness (top). The curve corresponds to the thickness distribution of FGC2200 at the horizontal line (bottom). (b–c) XRD patterns (b) and Raman spectra (c) of FGC at different heat treatment temperatures. D, G, and 2D peaks in panel c located at 1350, 1580, and 2700 cm<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. (d–f) HRTEM and SAED (top insets) images of different FGC. Bottom insets in panels d–f are schematic of structural evolution in FGC, where defect repairing and graphene stacking can be observed.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/asset/images/large/nl2c02177_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The crystalline structure of FGC after graphitization was further probed by XRD (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b). The diffraction peaks of FGC samples are consistent with hexagonal graphite (JCPDS card no. 89-8487). For FGC1400, the (002) peak contains a bulge at 25.8° and a sharp peak at 26.5°, indicating the transition of disordered carbon to graphitic carbon. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> In contrast, the appearance of single (002) peak at 26.3° suggests a structural rearrangement in FGC2200. This (002) peak becomes sharper in FGC3000, indicating higher crystallinity (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b). Moreover, Raman spectra were recorded to analyze the defects in FGC (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c). Specifically, <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio is well-used to judge the disordered degree of carbon materials, where D peak and G peak correspond to the vibration of the defective carbon and the in-plane stretching vibration of the C–C bond, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a> The significant decreasing of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio from 0.95 (FGC1400) to 0.2 (FGC2200) suggests that thermal treatment effectively reduces defects. The D peak and <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio of FGC3000 are almost unchanged, which can be attributed to the nearly completed defect repair in the 2200 °C heat treatment. Nitrogen adsorption and desorption isotherms also indicate that the pore structure in FGC decrease during graphitization (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>).</p><p>Furthermore, high-resolution TEM was used to trace the evolution of structural order during graphitization. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d, FGC1400 shows amorphous structure with few-layer turbostratic nanosheets. At higher temperatures, defect repairing and graphene stacking can be identified (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e–f). Specifically, the structural order gradually improved, that is, the parallel lattice stripes about 6 layers in FGC2200 can be observed, which is consistent with the calculated results via the ratio of the integrated intensities of G and 2D peaks in Raman spectra. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> And the thickening of FGC3000 (nearly 30 layers) can be explained by the inevitable stack when the temperature is high enough. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> This finding is also confirmed by the observing gradually clear diffraction spots in selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns (insets in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e,f). The detailed structural parameters of the FGC are listed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>.</p><p>The Na-storage behavior of different FGC samples was evaluated in the Na||FGC half coin cells with 1 M sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>) in diglyme as electrolyte. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a</a>, FGC1400 exhibited a typical slope-like curve in the galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) profiles, while clear ternary intercalation plateau region at ∼0.6 V in FGC2200 and FGC3000 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a</a>). This plateau can be further tailored via elevated temperature (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7b</a>), which is consistent with a previous report. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Clearly, a transition from electrochemical capacitance behavior to Faradaic intercalation is identified from FGC1400 to FGC2200/FGC3000. FGC1400 is amorphous with turbostratic carbon layers, and the resultant weak interaction between host and Na<sup>+</sup> ions inhibited the formation of GICs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> But for FGC2200/FGC3000, the highly ordered graphitic structure contributes to enhanced interaction, and thus the formation of GICs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> Notably, although the two FGC samples follow the same intercalation behavior, FGC3000 delivered a lower capacity. This may arise from stacking of more graphene layers in FGC3000 which results in formation of winkles and inactiving Na-storage sites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> However, apart from the reversible capacity, no apparent differences in cycle stability can be observed in three FGC samples during 1200 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7c</a>). Regarding to the thickness uniformity (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>) and the highest capacity (129 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), FGC2200 as the optimized sample will be further investigated.</p><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/asset/images/medium/nl2c02177_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/asset/images/large/nl2c02177_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Electrochemical performance of FGC2200 anodes in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> diglyme of Na half-cell within the voltage range of 0.01–2 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). (a) The second discharge/charge curves of FGC2200 at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Rate performances of FGC2200 at current density range from 0.1 to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) The cycling stability of FGC2200 and NG at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) The long-term cycle performances of FGC at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Considering the activation of anode under high rates, the maximal reversible capacity (charge capacity after 15 cycles at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>) is chosen as the initial capacity to calculate capacity retention rate. (e) Comparison of the cyclic stability and rate performance between FGC2200 and previously reported different carbon anodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/asset/images/large/nl2c02177_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>To evaluate the effect of few-layer nanostructure on high rate capability, fast-charging tests were carried out. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b, FGC2200 demonstrates a remarkable capacity retention up to near 82% even when the current density is increased by 100-folds from 0.1 to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Such a high capacity retention under fast-charging conditions has not been validated previously in SIB carbon anodes, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref12 ref20 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref8 ref12 ref20 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 6 to 8, 12, 20 and 28 to 31">(6−8,12,20,28−31)</a> which is also better than the other two FGC samples (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7d</a>). Note that FGC2200 only consists of nanosized few graphitic layers, which is quite unlike micrometer-sized bulk morphology for natural graphite (NG) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8c</a>), but it shares a comparable plateau-like intercalation feature with NG (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8a,b</a>). Thus, we chose conventional NG as a contrast sample. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c, compared with the high cyclic stability of FGC2200, the obvious capacity decay was observed in NG, which might be caused by the inferior ion diffusion. Furthermore, this superiority is magnified in the long term fast-charging test, where a 10 000-cycle cell with reversible capacity of 119 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> achieved at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Even at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, FGC2200 exhibited a high capacity retention of 94% after 8000 cycles with 0.0013% capacity loss per cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d). As summarized in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e, FGC2200 outperforms almost all reported carbons in fast-charging capability and long-term stability (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a> for detail comparison).</p><p>In situ XRD measurement was carried out to elucidate Na-storage mechanism of FGC2200. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a, during the discharge process, the main (002) peak of FGC2200 at 26.3° splits into two peaks, which can be assigned to the formation of high-staged graphite intercalation compounds (GICs). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> With the intercalation of solvated Na<sup>+</sup> ions, the two split peaks shift to opposite diffraction angles. At the end of discharge, the diffraction pattern evolves into three peaks of 15°, 22.7°, and 30.5°, typically representing the formation of stage-one ternary GICs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> These peaks can be indexed as (002), (003), and (004) of stage-one ternary GICs <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a> for the detailed calculation). This observation demonstrates that FGC2200 follows a classic ternary intercalation mechanism which NG also follows, where solvated Na<sup>+</sup> ions intercalate into the two adjacent graphitic layers. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref35" aria-label="reference 15 and 35">(15,35)</a> Meanwhile, this result explains the potential plateau observed in the sodiation profile (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a). Moreover, the recovery of (002) peak after two cycles of charging can be observed, suggesting a highly reversible intercalation/deintercalation process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a).</p><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/asset/images/medium/nl2c02177_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/asset/images/large/nl2c02177_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Na-storage mechanism of FGC2200 and NG. (a) In situ XRD patterns range from 12° to 37° of FGC2200 recorded in the first two discharge/charge. (b–d) In situ Raman spectra of NG and FGC2200. The color-mapped curves of NG (b) and FGC2200 (d) range from 1500 to 1650 cm<sup>–1</sup> in the second charge/discharge and magnified views of NG (c) and FGC2200 (e) in selected voltage range.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/asset/images/large/nl2c02177_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The correlation between microstructure and Na-storage mechanism can be further clarified by in situ Raman spectra. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c, the split of G peak is clear observed in discharge of the NG, where G peak at 1582 cm<sup>–1</sup> and G′ peak at 1599 cm<sup>–1</sup> can be identified. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref36" aria-label="reference 16 and 36">(16,36)</a> The split is driven by the phase evolution when increasing solvated Na<sup>+</sup> ions intercalated into carbon layers. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> This result indicates that the pristine graphitic carbon and sodiated carbon coexist in the initial discharge of NG, which can be assigned to the formation of high-staged ternary GICs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37,38)</a> Meanwhile, upon discharge from 0.82 to 0.66 V, the G′ peak gradually increased while the G peak is steadily weakened and finally disappeared (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d). In the latter discharge, the blue-shifting G′ peak from 1599 to 1604 cm<sup>–1</sup> occurred suggesting the transformation to full intercalated phase (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b). In contrast, FGC2200 exhibits a significantly different spectroscopic signature, that is, a sequential peak shift from 1580 to 1604 cm<sup>–1</sup> rather than coexistence of split peaks is observed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e. This observation corresponds to the short existence of high-staged GICs, which is similar to continuous intercalation of K<sup>+</sup> in carbon. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Specifically, few layers nanostructure in FGC2200 accounts for effective contact between solvated Na<sup>+</sup> ions and carbon layers. This drives the fast transition of high-staged GICs to low-staged GICs, and thus generates a sequential spectroscopic signature. More importantly, the short-lived high-staged GICs can reduce structural stress in nanosized FGC2200, which contributes to the high cyclic stability achieved at high rates.</p><p>To further analyze the difference in kinetics between FGC2200 and NG, cyclic voltammetry (CV) was carried out at different scan rates from 0.2 to 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. As observed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a,b, NG and FGC2200 showed clear cathodic peaks of stage-one GICs in the CV scan. These peaks in NG shifted to lower potentials upon scanning, which are probably affected by the increasing of polarization, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> while the peak positions in FGC2200 remained unchanged. Typically, the kinetics can be represented by <i>b</i> value in the CV power law, where the peak current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>) fit the equation of <i>i</i> = <i>av</i><sup><i>b</i></sup> (ref  <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a>). The calculated <i>b</i> value (<i>R</i> peak, stage-one GICs) for NG is 0.54 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c), indicating that the intercalation/deintercalation in NG is a diffusion-controlled process, which is consistent with previous reports. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 42 and 43">(42,43)</a></p><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/asset/images/medium/nl2c02177_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/asset/images/large/nl2c02177_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a,b) CV curves of NG (a) and FGC2200 (b) at various scan rates from 0.2 to 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. <i>R</i> in panel a, panel b is the cathodic peak of stage-one GICs. (c) <i>b</i> values of cathodic peak (<i>R</i> in a,b) in NG and FGC2200. (d) GITT curves and diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) of NG and FGC2200. (e) Schematic illustration of sodiation process of NG and FGC2200, including electrolyte wettability and ion diffusion between graphene layers.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/asset/images/large/nl2c02177_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>But interestingly, the <i>b</i> value of <i>R</i> (stage-one GICs) for FGC2200 is 0.92. This suggests that FGC2200 follows a surface-controlled kinetics, which is generally regarded as a typical electrochemical capacitance behavior. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> However, the intercalation behavior in FGC2200 is clearly identified in the sodiation profile (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b) and spectroscopic characterization (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>). Undoubtedly, a question arises, why does FGC2200 behave in a surface-controlled manner rather than the classic diffusion-controlled manner observed in NG? This seeming contradiction can be attributed to the difference in microstructure. The nanosized few layers account for the fast ion diffusion into the interlayer, which is distinguished with sluggish ion diffusion in micrometer-sized NG. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> This fast ion diffusion kinetics achieved in FGC2200 is further probed by the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d, the diffusion coefficient of FGC2200 is an order of magnitude higher than that of NG. Besides, the fitting data of electrochemical impendence spectroscopy (EIS) shows low charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) even after 200 cycles at 1A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>) indicating the favorable electrode kinetics. Therefore, an evident conclusion can be drawn that FGC2200 follows intercalation-type battery mechanism with fast ion diffusion kinetics, which is manifested as surface-controlled intercalation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 45 and 46">(45,46)</a></p><p>These findings are consistent with a recent study of cobalt oxide (Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>), where a battery-like storage behavior with electrochemical capacitance kinetics is observed. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> Meanwhile, FGC3000 also exhibited clear redox peaks with a high <i>b</i> value (0.92) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>), further corroborating this mechanism. This fast surface-controlled intercalation mechanism can be illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e. Compared with sluggish ion diffusion in NG, the nanosized architecture of FGC2200 enhanced the wettability of electrolyte and shorten the migration path of ions. On the other hand, the few-layer nanostructure with high crystallinity enables intercalation rather than electrochemical capacitance behavior. The better contact between solvated Na<sup>+</sup> ions and nanosized layers of FGC2200 is conductive to the efficient formation of stage-one GICs across graphene layers. Overall, from the kinetic perspective these results enrich the understanding of Na storage behavior in fast-charging carbon and advance the classical intercalation-type battery mechanism.</p><p>The practical application of FGC2200 was evaluated in full cells pairing with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathodes. The NVP cathodes delivered 106 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a plateau of 3.36 V at 100  and 78 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> over 200 cycles at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11a,b</a>. The discharge capacity of FGC2200||NVP full cells at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> was 108 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at first cycle and increased to 115 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after activation (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11c</a>). The low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) was 52%, which may originate from the solid electrolyte interphase on FGC2200 anode without presodiation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Interestingly, in contrast with the rapid capacity decay in NG, an impressive cycling stability was achieved in FGC2200 full cell even over 1400 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c02177/suppl_file/nl2c02177_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11d</a>), highlighting the potential for energy storage application.</p><p class="last">In summary, a few-layer graphitic carbon anode has been developed with excellent fast-charging capability (82% retention after current increasing by 100-fold) and cyclic stability (10 000-cycle at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>). Different from the diffusion-controlled intercalation in the conventional micrometer-sized NG, this 2D graphitic carbon enables intercalation of solvated Na<sup>+</sup> ion with fast surface-controlled kinetics. This finding refreshes the view of the classic Faradaic intercalation kinetics and highlights the importance of highly crystalline nanoscale structure in manipulating fast-charging performance. Besides, a fast transition of high-staged GICs to low-staged GICs can be identified in in situ Raman spectra, where a sequential spectroscopic signature is observed in this fast ion intercalation manner.</p><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title"                             cas_R

                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium/Potassium-Ion Batteries: Boosting the Rate Capability and Cycle Life by Combining Morphology, Defect and Structure Engineering</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Huang, Huijuan; Xu, Rui; Feng, Yuezhan; Zeng, Sifan; Jiang, Yu; Wang, Huijuan; Luo, Wei; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">32</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Carbon-based materials have been considered as the most promising anode materials for both sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (PIBs), owing to their good chem. stability, high elec. cond., and environmental benignity.  However, due to the large sizes of sodium and potassium ions, it is a great challenge to realize a carbon anode with high reversible capacity, long cycle life, and high rate capability.  Herein, by rational design, N-doped 3D mesoporous carbon nanosheets (N-CNS) are successfully synthesized, which can realize unprecedented electrochem. performance for both SIBs and PIBs.  The N-CNS possess an ultrathin nanosheet structure with hierarchical pores, ultrahigh level of pyridinic N/pyrrolic N, and an expanded interlayer distance.  The beneficial features that can enhance the Na-/K-ion intercalation/deintercalation kinetic process, shorten the diffusion length for both ions and electrons, and accommodate the vol. change are demonstrated.  Hence, the N-CNS-based electrode delivers a high capacity of 239 mAh g-1 at 5 A g-1 after 10 000 cycles for SIBs and 321 mAh g-1 at 5 A g-1 after 5000 cycles for PIBs.  First-principles calcn. shows that the ultrahigh doping level of pyridinic N/pyrrolic N contributes to the enhanced sodium and potassium storage performance by modulating the charge d. distribution on the carbon surface.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201803260"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ke, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ren, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiong, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qin, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mai, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Defect-Rich Soft Carbon Porous Nanosheets for Fast and High-Capacity Sodium-Ion Storage</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hard Carbon Nanosheets with Uniform Ultramicropores and Accessible Functional Groups Showing High Realistic Capacity and Superior Rate Performance for Sodium-Ion Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xia, Ji-Li; Yan, Dong; Guo, Li-Ping; Dong, Xiao-Ling; Li, Wen-Cui; Lu, An-Hui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon attracts considerable attention as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries; however, their poor rate capability and low realistic capacity have motivated intense research effort toward exploiting nanostructured carbons in order to boost their comprehensive performance.  Ultramicropores are considered essential for attaining high-rate capacity as well as initial Coulombic efficiency by allowing the rapid diffusion of Na+ and inhibiting the contact of the electrolyte with the inner carbon surfaces.  Herein, hard carbon nanosheets with centralized ultramicropores (≈ 0.5 nm) and easily accessible carbonyl groups (C=O)/hydroxy groups (O-H) are synthesized via interfacial assembly and carbonization strategies, delivering a large capacity (318 mA h g-1 at 0.02 A g-1), superior rate capability (145 mA h g-1 at 5.00 A g-1), and approx. 95% of reversible capacity &lt; 1.00 V.  Notably, a new charge model favoring fast capacitive sodium storage with dual potential plateaus is proposed.  That is, the deintercalation of Na+ from graphitic layers is manifested as the low-potential plateau region (0.01-0.10 V), contributing to stable insertion capacity; meanwhile, the surface desodiation process of the C=O and O-H groups corresponds to the high-potential plateau region (0.40-0.70 V), contributing to a fast capacitive storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201908159"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shao, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qi, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lian, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huo, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Multiple Active Sites of Carbon for High-Rate Surface-Capacitive Sodium-Ion Storage</span>. <i>Angew.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Multiple Active Sites of Carbon for High-Rate Surface-Capacitive Sodium-Ion Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Guang; Shao, Meng; Ding, Huarui; Qi, Ying; Lian, Jiabiao; Li, Sheng; Qiu, Jingxia; Li, Huaming; Huo, Fengwei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Although sodium ion batteries (SIBs) possess many beneficial features, their rate performance, cycling stability, and safety need improvement for com. applications.  Based on the mechanisms of the sodium ions storage in carbon materials, herein we present a multiple active sites decorated amorphous carbon (MAC) with rich structural defects and heteroatom doping as an anode material for SIBs.  The full utilization of fast bonding-debonding processes between the active sites and sodium ions could bring a capacitive strategy to achieve superior sodium storage properties.  Consequently, after materials characterization and electrochem. evaluation, the as-prepd. electrode could deliver high rate and long-life performance.  This active-site-related design could be extended to other types of electrode materials, thereby contributing to future practical SIB applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201903280"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nanda, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kwon, S.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High Capacity Adsorption-Dominated Potassium and Sodium Ion Storage in Activated Crumpled Graphene</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lee, Byeongyong; Kim, Myeongjin; Kim, Sunkyung; Nanda, Jagjit; Kwon, Seok Joon; Jang, Hee Dong; Mitlin, David; Lee, Seung Woo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">17</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1903280</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Structurally and chem. defective activated-crumbled graphene (A-CG) is employed to achieve unique synergy of large reversible potassium (K) and sodium (Na) ion storage capacity with fast charging and extended cyclability.  A-CG synthesis consists of low temp. spraying of graphene oxide slurry, followed by partial redn. annealing and air activation.  For K storage, the reversible capacities are 340 mAh g-1 at 0.04 A g-1, 261 mAh g-1 at 0.5 A g-1, and 210 mAh g-1 at 2 A g-1.  For Na storage, the reversible capacities are 280 mAh g-1 at 0.04 A g-1, 191 mAh g-1 at 0.5 A g-1, and 151 mAh g-1 at 2 A g-1.  A-CG shows a stable intermediate rate (0.5 Ag-1) cycling with both K and Na, with minimal fade after 2800 and 8000 cycles.  These are among the most favorable capacity-rate capability-cyclability combinations recorded for potassium-ion battery and sodium-ion battery carbons.  Electroanal. studies (cyclic voltammetry, galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique, b-value) and d. functional theory (DFT) reveal that enhanced electrochem. performance originates from ion adsorption at various defects, such as Stone-Wales defects.  Moreover, DFT highlights enhanced thermodn. stability of A-CG with adsorbed K vs. with adsorbed Na, explaining the unexpected higher reversible capacity with the former.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.0c14611"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Boosting Capacitive Sodium-Ion Storage in Electrochemically Exfoliated Graphite for Sodium-Ion Capacitors</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                 <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium (Na)-ion capacitors possess higher energy d. than supercapacitors and higher power d. than Na-ion batteries.  However, kinetic mismatches between fast capacitive charge storage on the cathode and sluggish battery-type reactions on the anode lead to a poor charge/discharge rate capability and insufficient power output of Na-ion capacitors.  Thus, developing suitable anode materials for Na-ion capacitors is urgently desirable.  This work demonstrates an electrochem. exfoliated graphite (EEG) anode with enhanced capacitive charge storage, greatly boosting the Na-ion reaction kinetics of co-intercalation.  The EEG anode shows a high reversible capacity of 109 mAh g-1 and maintains a good capacity retention of 90% after 1000 cycles.  The assembled Na-ion capacitor using the EEG anode can finish the charge/discharge process in less than 10 s, which achieves an ultrahigh power d. of 17,500 W kg-1 with an energy d. of 17 Wh kg-1.  The high capacitive contributions at both the anode and cathode contribute to the fast rate capability and high power output of the fabricated Na-ion capacitors.  This work will promote the development of ultrafast charging sodium-ion storage devices.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201700104"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ju, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xing, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xi, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiong, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Enhanced Capacity and Rate Capability of Nitrogen/Oxygen Dual-Doped Hard Carbon in Capacitive Potassium-Ion Storage</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Boosting the sodium storage behaviors of carbon materials in ether-based electrolyte through the artificial manipulation of microstructure</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiao, Wei; Sun, Qian; Liu, Jian; Xiao, Biwei; Liu, Yulong; Glans, Per-Anders; Li, Jun; Li, Ruying; Li, Xifei; Guo, Jinghua; Yang, Wanli; Sham, Tsun-Kong; Sun, Xueliang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
